        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644"><meta property="og:title" content="Multiple Strategies to Build High-Performance Spherical Na-Ion Layered Oxide Cathodes"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/acs.nanolett.4c02644.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="The development prospect of layered transition metal oxides in sodium-ion batteries is excellent, but there are some problems, such as poor cycle stability and a complex phase transition. The spherical NaNi0.25Fe0.15Mn0.3Ti0.1Sn0.05Co0.05Li0.1O2 (SP-HEO) has been developed to address the challenges faced by O3-type layered oxide in sodium-ion batteries. The SP-HEO material is synthesized by piling and high entropy. The multiple strategies combine to enhance the electrochemical performance and air stability. The SP-HEO demonstrated a specific discharge capacity of 150.1 mA h g–1 at 0.1 C and 100.4 mA h g–1 at 7 C. Ex situ XRD analysis confirmed that the SP-HEO effectively retards complex phase transitions. This study not only introduces a high-entropy design for high tap density spherical storage materials but also dispels industry concerns regarding the performance of sodium ion layered oxide cathode materials."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/acs.nanolett.4c02644.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187f59db99075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187f59db99075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '8f529d0f-7614-4e68-a940-ff97c408e6c7'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187f59db99075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-getftr-references-enabled="data-getFTR-references-enabled" data-getftr-reference-url="/action/CitationEntitlement" data-getftr-citedby-enabled="data-getFTR-citedBy-enabled" data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.4c02644&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a href="#" title="Close" aria-label="close" tabindex="0" class="figure-viewer_close icon-close"></a><h2 class="figure-viewer_label"><span class="figure-viewer_current"></span></h2><div class="figure-viewer_image"><a href="#" title="Next" class="figure-viewer_next icon-angle-right" style="display: inline;"></a><a href="#" title="Previous" class="figure-viewer_prev icon-angle-left" title="Download Hi-Res Image" target="_blank" class="figure-viewer_hi-res-link"><i class="icon-file-image-o"></i><span>Download Hi-Res Image</span></a><span class="fv-separator"></span><a href="#" href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span <script nonce="93187f59db99075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Multiple Strategies to Build High-Performance Spherical Na-Ion Layered Oxide Cathodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Multiple Strategies to Build High-Performance Spherical Na-Ion Layered Oxide Cathodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Multiple Strategies to Build High-Performance Spherical Na-Ion Layered Oxide Cathodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Multiple Strategies to Build High-Performance Spherical Na-Ion Layered Oxide Cathodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Multiple Strategies to Build High-Performance Spherical Na-Ion Layered Oxide Cathodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Multiple Strategies to Build High-Performance Spherical Na-Ion Layered Oxide Cathodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Multiple Strategies to Build High-Performance Spherical Na-Ion Layered Oxide Cathodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Multiple Strategies to Build High-Performance Spherical Na-Ion Layered Oxide Cathodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Multiple Strategies to Build High-Performance Spherical Na-Ion Layered Oxide Cathodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/medium/nl4c02644_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/large/nl4c02644_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">The development prospect of layered transition metal oxides in sodium-ion batteries is excellent, but there are some problems, such as poor cycle stability and a complex phase transition. The spherical NaNi<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.05</sub>Co<sub>0.05</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (SP-HEO) has been developed to address the challenges faced by O3-type layered oxide in sodium-ion batteries. The SP-HEO material is synthesized by piling and high entropy. The multiple strategies combine to enhance the electrochemical performance and air stability. The SP-HEO demonstrated a specific discharge capacity of 150.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C and 100.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 7 C. Ex situ XRD analysis confirmed that the SP-HEO effectively retards complex phase transitions. This study not only introduces a high-entropy design for high tap density spherical storage materials but also dispels industry concerns regarding the performance of sodium ion layered oxide cathode materials.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D14924%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DXiangnan%2BLi%252C%2BXinyu%2BTang%252C%2BMengdan%2BZhang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D24%26issueNum%3D47%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.4c02644%26title%3DMultiple%2BStrategies%2Bto%2BBuild%2BHigh-Performance%2BSpherical%2BNa-Ion%2BLayered%2BOxide%2BCathodes%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D14931%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291495&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Atmospheric chemistry"> Atmospheric chemistry </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials"> Materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides"> Oxides </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290776&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Phase transitions"> Phase transitions </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292316&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transition metals"> Transition metals </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Entropy&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20entropy">high entropy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Spherical+Layered+Oxide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_spherical%20layered%20oxide">spherical layered oxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Prilling&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_prilling">prilling</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><p class="first">The development and utilization of clean energy, such as wind and solar energy, have spurred the advancement of efficient and secure energy storage applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Among the various types of energy storage technologies emerging, sodium-ion batteries are being considered as a viable supplement and alternative to lithium-ion batteries, owing to their rich sodium reserves and cost-effectiveness. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4−6)</a> Positive electrode materials play a crucial role in the performance of sodium-ion batteries, with materials like layered transition metal oxides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a> Prussian blue analogues, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> and polyanion frameworks <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11,12)</a> being extensively studied. Among these materials, the O3 layered oxide stands out as particularly promising due to its straightforward synthesis process, high capacity, dense structure, and greater sodium ion content. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> However, during the charge–discharge cycle, the material experiences an irreversible phase transition caused by the comparatively larger size of sodium ions, leading to structural instability and eventual loss of capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15−17)</a></p><p>High entropy oxides are multicomponent single-phase solid solutions, where each ingredient’s composition varies from 5% to 30%, and many elements share crystal positions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 18 to 20">(18−20)</a> The main structure is stabilized by the entropy stabilization effect. These high entropy oxides have garnered significant attention for their exceptional properties in fabricating positive materials for sodium-ion batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Researchers have explored the potential of high entropy oxides in this domain with promising results. Ding et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> utilized a high entropy configuration to design NaNi<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, demonstrating excellent long cycling performance and high exothermic temperature characteristics. Another notable study by Zhang et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> involved the construction of a high entropy oxide with a superstructure NaMg<sub>0.08</sub>Cu<sub>0.12</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. This material achieved a discharge-specific capacity of 131 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and capacity retention of 84% after 200 cycles at a high cutoff voltage of 4.3 V. Furthermore, the high entropy effect has been shown to significantly mitigate issues related to irreversible and complex phase transitions that occur in layered oxides during the charging and discharging processes.</p><p>In this work, we introduce Ni, Fe, Mn, Ti, Sn, Co and Li into the transition metal layer and for the first time synthesize a spherical O3-type high entropy oxide, NaNi<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.05</sub>Co<sub>0.05</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. It bases on the characteristics of the synergistic effect of various elements in high entropy oxides. Creatively utilizing the prilling method, our approach differs from traditional high-temperature solid-state, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> sol–gel <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> and coprecipitation methods <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> for the synthesis of high entropy materials. The materials exhibit the spherical structure, and nanoscale primary particles effectively reduce the diffusion energy barrier of sodium ions, thereby improving material properties. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a> The multiple strategies demonstrated that SP-HEO exhibits excellent air stability, higher rate performance, and tap density.</p><p>NaNi<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.05</sub>Co<sub>0.05</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials were synthesized through prilling. Stoichiometric amounts of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (Macklin, 99.9%), Co<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (Macklin, AR), NiO (Bidepharm 99.5%), and MnO<sub>2</sub> (Macklin, 99%). SnO (Macklin, 99.5%) and TiO<sub>2</sub> (Aladdin, 99.8%) were sanded and mixed with deionized water at 2000 rm/min. The slurry was converted into a powder precursor through a spray dryer. Then powder precursor and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Macklin, 99.9%), Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Aladdin, 99%) were calcined at 900, 950, and 980 °C for 10 h in the air. The material was sintered at 950 °C, which was named SP-HEO. The NaNi<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.05</sub>Co<sub>0.05</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> material was synthesized by wet ball milling and sintered at 900 °C, naming HEO. Finally, cooling to room temperature, the obtained black powders were stored in an Ar-filled glovebox quickly to avoid them being contaminated by moisture. The details of materials characterization, Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient and electrochemical measurements are described in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</p><p>The principal characteristic peaks of HEO are all indexed to the O3 structure with the <i>R</i><span class="overline">3</span><i>m</i> group (PDF: #54-0887) depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a. The morphology of HEO reveals a consistent layered structure with a thickness of roughly 500 nm to 1 μm in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b. The cycle and rate performances of HEO are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c,d. The specific discharge capacity is 154.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C and 143.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 C. Furthermore, the rate performance is tested and shows specific discharge capacities of 106 and 94.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 and 7 C. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e, the oxidation/reduction peaks at 2.95/2.7, 3.1/2.9 and 3.6/3.5 V can be attributed to redox couples of Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup>, respectively. The calculated values of <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> for the charge and discharge process are 1.722 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and 1.827 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f. After exposure, peaks corresponding to Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>·nH<sub>2</sub>O (PDF: #72-0578) are observed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g, indicating the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>·nH<sub>2</sub>O due to the reaction between the Na<sup>+</sup> ions escaping from the lattice and the H<sub>2</sub>O/CO<sub>2</sub> in the air. The SEM images of the exposed HEO material reveal the maintenance of its layered structure without any obvious change, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>h. Furthermore, the initial specific discharge capacity of the exposed HEO materials is 118.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity retention of 78% after air exposure depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>i.</p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/medium/nl4c02644_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/large/nl4c02644_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns and (b) SEM of HEO. Electrochemical performances of HEO cathode. (c) Long-term cycling performance at 0.5 C between 2.0 and 4.3 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). (d) Rate performance at different rates. (e) CV profiles in the first three cycles at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (f) GITT curves for the charge and discharge states of the first cycle and corresponding <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>. Air stability of HEO material. (g) XRD patterns of the exposed and unexposed materials. (h) SEM images of the exposed material. (i) Cycling performance at 0.5 C. Reproduced from ref  <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a>. Copyright 2024 American Chemical Society.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/large/nl4c02644_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>In order to improve rate performance and air stability, the spherical high entropy cathode material is synthesized by the prilling method. The crystal structures of SP-HEO sintered at various temperatures are analyzed using X-ray diffraction (XRD), and the results are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The XRD patterns are found to align closely with the O3-type structure within the R<span class="overline">3</span>m space group (PDF: #54-0887) and no impurity phases are detected. Further, the Rietveld refinement is used to determine the information on the crystal structure in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a, yielding refined lattice parameters of <i>a</i> = 2.9764 Å and <i>c</i> = 16.0765 Å (as detailed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, with additional Rietveld refinement data provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). The morphology of SP-HEO is examined using scanning electron microscopy (SEM), revealing a spherical structure with a particle size of 12 μm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2</a>), indicating the successful synthesis of spherical cathode materials through prilling. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping techniques are employed for further characterization, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c-e. Specifically, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d exhibits continuous lattice fringes with a width of 2.09 Å, corresponding to crystal planes (015). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> The bright diffraction points are linked to crystal planes (012) and (009) in selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns. Moreover, EDS spectra show that the Na, Ni, Fe, Mn, Ti, Sn, Co, and O elements are uniformly distributed in the material in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f. The regular spherical particles vastly improve the tap density in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>; the tap density of SP-HEO is significantly higher than that of HEO, indicating that prilling can improve the tap density of materials.</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/medium/nl4c02644_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/large/nl4c02644_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Characterization of the structure and morphology of SP-HEO. (a) Rietveld refinement of X-ray diffraction. (b) SEM image. (c, d) TEM image. (e) SAED pattern from the zone axis of [100]. (f) TEM-EDS mapping.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/large/nl4c02644_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>Furthermore, the XPS measurements of SP-HEO are conducted to investigate the valence states in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>. Fe<sup>3+</sup> is attributed to the significant peaks of Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> at 711 and 724 eV, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> respectively. The principal peaks of Ni 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ni 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, located at 855 and 872 eV, are attributed to Ni<sup>2+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The major peaks of Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, located at 642.3 and 653.7 eV, are attributed to Mn<sup>4+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The entire XPS spectrum identifies the signal peaks of every element, indicating the introduction of high-entropy elements into the lattice structure. The significant peaks at 780.13 and 794.87 eV correspond to Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> spectra, which are attributed to Co<sup>3+</sup>. Similarly, the primary peaks of Sn 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Sn 3d<sub>5/2</sub> are located at 493.8 and 485.6 eV, respectively. That is indicative of Sn<sup>4+</sup>. In the case of Ti<sup>4+</sup>, the significant peaks at 457.5 and 463.4 eV conform to Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub> spectra. All elements’ signal peaks can be identified in the XPS full spectrum, proving that high-entropy elements are introduced into the lattice structure. The presence of metal–oxygen bonds at 529.8 eV can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>i. Sodium atoms combine with oxygen atoms to form the alkaline metal layer. These elements (Ni, Fe, Mn, Ti, Sn, Co, Li) share crystallographic sites and combine with oxygen atoms to form metal–oxygen bonds and to build the transition metal layer in layered transition oxides, which can stabilize the host lattice in their solid-solution state through the entropy-stabilization effect. The layered transition metal oxide is constructed by the alkali metal layer and the transition metal layer together.</p><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/medium/nl4c02644_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/large/nl4c02644_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. XPS measurement results of SP-HEO. (a) The survey spectra, (b) Ni 2p spectra, (c) Fe 2p spectra, (d) Mn 2p spectra, (e) Ti 2p spectra, (f) Sn 3d spectra, (g) Co 2p spectra, (h) C 1s spectra, (i) O 1s spectra.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/large/nl4c02644_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The electrochemical performance of SP-HEO is evaluated in the coin-type half cells with sodium metal as the anode at 2.0–4.3 V in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>. The cycle and rate performances of materials with different synthesis temperatures are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. The initial charge–discharge curves at 0.1 C (1 C = 180 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. SP-HEO demonstrates a specific capacity of 150.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C and 141.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 C. After 200 cycles, the discharge capacity of SP-HEO is 114.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and higher than that of HEO with 109.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The specific initial discharge capacities of SP-HEO at 5  and 7 C corresponded to 106.5 and 100.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b, respectively. SP-HEO demonstrates excellent rate performance and cycling stability. The rate performance of SP-HEO is higher than HEO in high current density. Compared to other high-entropy layered oxide materials, the rate performance of SP-HEO is found to be at a high level, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c for the first three cycles at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> attribute the oxidation/reduction peaks at 4.2/4.3 V to the oxygen redox reaction. The oxidation/reduction peaks at 2.88/2.71, 3.12/3.08 and 3.6/3.5 V can be attributed to redox couples of Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup>. Subsequent cycles exhibit a stabilization of the oxygen redox peak intensity, indicating a good reversibility.</p><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/medium/nl4c02644_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/large/nl4c02644_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performances of SP-HEO cathode. (a) Long-term cycling performance at 0.5 C. (b) Rate performance at different rates. (c) CV profiles in the first three cycles at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (d) EIS spectra and the corresponding equivalent circuit after 50 cycles. (e) Low-temperature electrical performance at 0.1 C (f) GITT curves for the charge and discharge states of the first cycle and corresponding <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/large/nl4c02644_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>After 3, 50, and 100 cycles, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) is conducted on the SP-HEO materials. The equivalent circuit, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, is formulated as a simplified interfacial model. Within this model, <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> represents the ohmic resistance. <i>R</i><sub>sf</sub> is denoted as the interfacial impedance of Na<sup>+</sup> across the cathode-electrolyte interface (CEI) in the high-frequency zone, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> is denoted as the charge transfer impedance in the low-frequency region. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Furthermore, the portrayal of the low-frequency tail’s dominant Warburg impedance is encapsulated by <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>, signifying mass transfer processes. The extensive fitting values for the impedance parameters can be found in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a>. The electrochemical impedance of SP-HEO is smaller than that of the HEO.</p><p>The electrical performances of SP-HEO at low temperatures are examined, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e. SP-HEO maintains a specific discharge capacity of 103.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at −20 °C. The low-temperature performance of SP-HEO is close to that of HEO. The two materials exhibit excellent low-temperature electrochemical performance stability. These results suggest that high-entropy material can operate in harsh environments.</p><p>To delve deeper into the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup>, the galvanostatic intermittent titration method (GITT) is employed as in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f. Specifically, the measurements are performed at charge and discharge rates of 0.1 C within a voltage range of 2.0–4.3 V. The resulting calculated diffusion coefficients for Na<sup>+</sup> are 1.855 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> during the charging process and 1.38 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> during the discharge process. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> demonstrates that layer spacing of SP-HEO is larger than nonhigh-entropy layered oxides NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. SP-HEO shows a higher rate performance. Compared to nonhigh-entropy layered oxides materials, these findings underscore the efficacy of the high-entropy. And it can enhance electrochemical performance through the expansion of Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels.</p><p>Ex situ XRD analysis is performed to investigate the phase transitions of SP-HEO during the charge–discharge process in the voltage range  2.0–4.3 V at 0.1 C. The material maintained an O3 structure before charging to 4.3 V as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a-c. Upon charging to 4.3 V, the only observed phase transformation is from O3 to O3′, indicating the exceptional electrochemical stability of SP-HEO in high-voltage regimes. Conversely, during the discharging process, the O3′ phase reverted to the O3 phase, highlighting a reputably reversible transition. The diagram in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c visually presents the phase evolution during the charging process. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d-f, the phase transitions of HEO are that of O3–P3–OP2. It shows that prilling can retard the complexity of phase transformation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 37 to 39">(37−39)</a></p><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/medium/nl4c02644_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/large/nl4c02644_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Ex situ XRD pattern, (b) the charge–discharge curve of SP-HEO at 0.1C. (c) Demonstration diagram of phase transition in the charging process of SP-HEO. (d) XRD patterns, (e) the charge–discharge curve of HEO at 0.1 C. (f) Demonstration diagram of phase transition in the charging process of HEO. Reproduced from ref  <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a>. Copyright 2024 American Chemical Society. (g) XRD patterns of SP-HEO materials after 100 cycles. (h) XRD patterns of SP-HEO for exposure to air for 10 days. (i) The cycle performance of SP-HEO and HEO for exposure to air for 10 days.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/asset/images/large/nl4c02644_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The SP-HEO material exhibits high stability during the charge and discharge processes by the high-entropy effect. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g further confirms the viewpoints, before and after 100 cycle, characteristic peaks indexed to the O3 structure with the <i>R</i><span class="overline">3</span><i>m</i> group, indicating the stability of the material structure. SEM images according to <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S7</a> illustrate that after 100 cycles the material retains its spherical structure without any signs of structural collapse or damage.</p><p>Concerns surrounding the air stability of layered transition metal oxide include the spontaneous extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> leading to reduced specific discharge capacity and increased production, storage and transportation costs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 40 to 44">(40−44)</a> To evaluate the air stability of the SP-HEO material, the sample is exposed to air for 10 days at room temperature and approximately 40% humidity. The air humidity statistics at room temperature are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S7</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h confirms that no impurity phases are detected in the diffraction peak after exposure. SEM images in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> reveal that the layered structure of the SP-HEO material remained intact without any visible cracks. The initial specific discharge capacity of the exposed SP-HEO material is 136.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, with a capacity retention of 91% before exposure, as demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>i. Compared with other materials in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644/suppl_file/nl4c02644_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S6</a>, SP-HEO underlines the effectiveness of high-entropy design in synthesizing cathode materials and its contribution to enhancing air stability.</p><p class="last">The spherical NaNi<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.05</sub>Co<sub>0.05</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials have been developed by high-entropy design and a prilling cooperative strategy. Through prilling, we have developed a spherical high-entropy O3-type layered transition metal oxide. This high-entropy arrangement has notably enhanced the physical and chemical properties of the material. The spherical structure plays a key role in shortening the diffusion distance of sodium ions. Consequently, the complex phase transitions observed during the charge and discharge process are effectively restrained. Notably, this high-energy oxide (SP-HEO) exhibits excellent air stability, the higher rate performance and tap density. The investigations suggest that the multiple strategies present the novel approach for synthesizing high-performance cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c02644</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD patterns of the materials synthesized by prilling sintered in different temperatures; The SEM images of materials synthesized by prilling sintered in 900 and 980 °C; Cycling performance of materials synthesized by prilling at 0.5 C after preactivation at 0.1 C for first three cycles; The initial charge–discharge curves of SP-HEO at 0.1 C between 2.0 and 4.3 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na); EIS spectra and the corresponding equivalent circuit of SP-HEO after 3 and 100 cycles; XRD patterns and rate performance of SP-HEO, HEO and NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>; SEM image of SP-HEO after 100 cycles; SEM images of exposed SP-HEO material; Crystallographic and Rietveld refinement data of the HEO cathode; Calculated lattice parameters of SP-HEO; Calculated lattice parameters of SP-HEO, HEO and NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>;                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ti-Gradient Doping to Stabilize Layered Surface Structure for High Performance High-Ni Oxide Cathode of Li-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kong, Defei; Hu, Jiangtao; Chen, Zhefeng; Song, Kepeng; Li, Cheng; Weng, Mouyi; Li, Maofan; Wang, Rui; Liu, Tongchao; Liu, Jiajie; Zhang, Mingjian; Xiao, Yinguo; Pan, Feng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">41</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1901756</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-Ni layered oxide cathodes are considered to be one of the most promising cathodes for high-energy-d. lithium-ion batteries due to their high capacity and low cost.  However, surfice residues, such as NiO-type rock-salt phase and Li2CO3, are often formed at particle surface due to the high reactivity of Ni3+, and inevitably result in an inferior electrochem. performance, hindering the practical application.  Herein, unprecedentedly clean surfaces without any surfice residues are obtained in a representative LiNi0.8Co0.2O2 cathode by Ti-gradient doping.  High-resoln. transmission electron microscopy (TEM) reveals that the particle surface is composed of a disordered layered phase (~ 6 nm in thickness) with the same rhombohedra structure as its interior.  The formation of this disordered layered phase at particle surface is electrochem. favored.  It leads to the highest rate capacity ever reported and a superior cycling stability.  First-principles calcns. further confirm that excellent electrochem. performance has roots in excellent chem./structural stability of such a disordered layered structure, mainly arising from the improved robustness of the oxygen framework by Ti doping.  This strategy of constructing the disordered layered phase at the particle surface could be extended to other high-Ni layered transition metal oxides, which will contribute to enhancement of their electrochem. performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201902698"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ryu, H.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, K.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoon, D.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Li[Ni0.9Co0.09W0.01]O2: A New Type of Layered Oxide Cathode with High Cycling Stability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ryu, Hoon-Hee; Park, Kang-Joon; Yoon, Dae Ro; Aishova, Assylzat; Yoon, Chong S.; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">44</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1902698</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Substituting W for Al in the Ni-rich cathode Li[Ni0.885Co0.10Al0.015]O2 (NCA89) produces Li[Ni0.9Co0.09W0.01]O2 (NCW90) with markedly reduced primary particle size.  Particle size refinement considerably improves the cathode's cycling stability such that the NCW90 cathode retains 92% of its initial capacity after 1000 cycles (compared to 63% for NCA89), while the cathode produces a high initial discharge capacity of 231.2 mAh g-1 (at 0.1 C).  Thus, the proposed NCW90 can deliver high energy d. and a long battery lifetime simultaneously, unlike other Ni-rich layered oxide cathodes.  This unprecedented cycling stability is mainly attributed to a series of interparticular microfractures that absorb the anisotropic lattice strain caused by a deleterious phase transition near the charge end, thereby improving the cathode's resistance to fracture.  Microcrack suppression preserves the mech. integrity of the cathode particles during cycling and protects the particle interior from detrimental electrolyte attack.  The proposed NCW90 cathode provides an improved material from which a new series of Ni-rich layered cathode can be developed for next-generation elec. vehicles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/tcr.201700057"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hosaka, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kumakura, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Towards K-Ion and Na-Ion Batteries as “Beyond Li-Ion</span>. <i>Chem.                 10.1002/tcr.201700057</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D18%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D459-479%26journal%3DChem.%2BRec.%26issue%3D4%26author%3DK.%2BKubota%26author%3DM.%2BDahbi%26author%3DT.%2BHosaka%26author%3DS.%2BKumakura%26author%3DS.%2BKomaba%26title%3DTowards%2BK-Ion%2Band%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%2Bas%2B%25E2%2580%259CBeyond%2BLi-Ion%26doi%3D10.1002%252Ftcr.201700057&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.4c02644&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Ftcr.201700057&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards K-Ion and Na-Ion Batteries as "Beyond Li-Ion"</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kubota, Kei; Dahbi, Mouad; Hosaka, Tomooki; Kumakura, Shinichi; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Record</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Li-ion battery commercialized by Sony in 1991 has the highest energy-d. among practical rechargeable batteries and is widely used in electronic devices, elec. vehicles, and stationary energy storage system in the world.  Moreover, the battery market is rapidly growing in the world and further fast-growing is expected.  With expansion of the demand and applications, price of lithium and cobalt resources is increasing.  We are, therefore, motivated to study Na- and K-ion batteries for stationary energy storage system because of much abundant Na and K resources and the wide distribution in the world.  In this account, we review developments of Na- and K-ion batteries with mainly introducing our previous and present researches in comparison to that of Li-ion battery.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.-K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1007/s40820-022-00844-2"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Energy Batteries: Beyond Lithium-Ion and Their Long Road to Commercialisation</span>. <i>Nano-Micro Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>14</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">1</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">94</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-energy batteries: beyond lithium-ion and their long road to commercialisation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gao, Yulin; Pan, Zhenghui; Sun, Jianguo; Liu, Zhaolin; Wang, John</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano-Micro Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">94</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NLAEBV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2150-5551</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nano-Micro Letters</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Rechargeable batteries of high energy d. and overall performance are becoming a critically important technol. in the rapidly changing society of the twenty-first century.  While lithium-ion batteries have so far been the dominant choice, numerous emerging applications call for higher capacity, better safety and lower costs while maintaining sufficient cyclability.  The design space for potentially better alternatives is extremely large, with numerous new chemistries and architectures being simultaneously explored.  These include other insertion ions (e.g. sodium and numerous multivalent ions), conversion electrode materials (e.g. silicon, metallic anodes, halides and chalcogens) and aq. and solid electrolytes.  However, each of these potential "beyond lithium-ion" alternatives faces numerous challenges that often lead to very poor cyclability, esp. at the com. cell level, while lithium-ion batteries continue to improve in performance and decrease in cost.  This review examines fundamental principles to rationalise these numerous developments, and in each case, a brief overview is given on the advantages, advances, remaining challenges preventing cell-level implementation and the state-of-the-art of the solns. to these challenges.  Finally, research and development results obtained in academia are compared to emerging com. examples, as a commentary on the current and near-future viability of these "beyond lithium-ion" alternatives.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202311816"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Earth-Abundant Fe-Mn-Based Compound Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Challenges and Progress</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structure evolution of layered transition metal oxide cathode materials for Na-ion batteries: Issues, mechanism and strategies</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Yanshuo; Liu, Qi; Zhao, Xiaohan; Mu, Daobin; Tan, Guoqiang; Li, Li; Chen, Renjie; Wu, Feng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Today (Oxford, United Kingdom)</span>
